And let your position be such when you place the member over an equal place according to its natural shape, until the parts of the fractured member extend; then strive to bring back those additions with all skill and in whatever way you can at their places: and beware according to your strength, lest pain occur in a weak person due to your operation, nor injury; and strive according to your strength to apply one of two bones with its partner, according to the best shapes.
And it is necessary that at that hour you try and touch it (the fractured member) with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it and equalize it according to the quantity of your force.
And beware of violent extension and strong compression, as many foolish ones do. For many times they cause boils to appear due to their operation or a contraction in the member; as we have already seen from their operations many times.
Then attach after equalization, and a proper operation, and constriction of that member, and calmness, and peace; and warn the weak one about it. Make him be careful not to move himself during his hours of vigil, and during the turning of his sleep, and during his agitations, and during his digestions, and all his movements with utmost strength. Make him be careful that the position of a member will not follow pain. Because when he feels pain or injury in the disposition of a member's position, then it is necessary to change his position into another that does not cause him pain.
Be careful that his position is equal and straight so that it does not happen that there will be deformation in the member when it is restored.
And the quality of the tightening of a broken member, as I said.
Know that broken members are diversified according to their size, and the magnitude of their parts, and their form.
Therefore, those that are small, such as the arm, and finger, and tool, and similar to them, then it is necessary for there to be a soft, subtle wrapping of cloth around them. But those that are large, such as the thigh and leg, it is necessary for there to be a wide, firm wrapping around them.
Because a broad bandage connects a large member and tightly binds it from all sides with an equal tightness, so that no spot is left open.
Then after your evacuation, begin to apply a suitable liniment on it, on a soft woolen cloth over the place of the fracture. Then begin to wrap the bandage around the place of the fracture three or four times, according to what the member deserves, and tighten your hand slightly with the bandage.
Then go with him to the upper part from the place of the fracture, and tighten less than your previous tightening that you had at the fractured place. Then gradually extend yourself from the place of the fracture a little, and soften the constriction slowly, until you take out a large piece from a healthy place.
Then take another plaster and apply it again over the fractured place with folds. Then go downwards with folds towards the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in the constriction of the fold and its softening, as we said in the first fold above.
Then put between the folds from soft wool or cloth that corresponds to the twisting of the fracture if it is twisted: and if not, do not place anything there.
Then fold again over the other plaster. Then place stars evenly on these folds immediately, if there is no swelling or hot abscess.
If there is a swelling or an abscess there: then place on it what sits on that abscess and remove the swelling; and leave it for some days. Then tie the stars over it.
And let the stars be from the middle parts of wide canes, shaved, well-formed: or let them be from wood where sieves are made, which are made from pine, fir, or palm branches, or boxwood: and let the width of each star be according to this form and this same shape.
However, the star that is placed over the fracture should be slightly larger and wider than the other stars: and the length of the star should be according to what fits the limb in size and smallness.
Then tie with another plaster over the stars according to the first constriction. Then tie above with cords evenly, as we said about the constriction: and let your constriction be over the fracture place more: and the more it is extended from the fracture, the less the constriction.
And it is necessary that the threads are medium-sized and fine, and they should be made from soft linen.
If the threads are thick, as I have seen stupid people using twisted threads of coarse linen in their operations, it is a great mistake: because the constriction falls out with them beyond its quality.
And fine threads again do not agree: because you will not achieve with them by constriction what you want.
It is not necessary that there be less than a finger's breadth between the astellae (metal needles or pins used in surgery).
If the sick person is hurt by the tips of the astellae after constriction in healthy places: then place beneath them with soft wool or camouflaged wool until nothing is hurt from it.
When there is a fracture with wound, and it happens that the break-up occurs on the skin, I shall come again with its reminder to the second chapter of singularity (specific cases).
And know that not every broken member must be bound with pins from the first day. That is, because when the member is large, it is not necessary to place needles above it, unless after five days or seven or more, according to your safety from the occurrence of an abscess from a hot wound.
This is the method of plasters which the ancients used to restore that are placed on a fracture and dislocation.
Common emplaster method abbreviated, restoring the fracture, suitable for multiple temperaments, especially women and children, because it is from that which heat does not conquer nor cold.
And it is that you should take from the flour mill dust, and it is the marrow of the wheat that hangs on the walls of the mill near the movement of the grinder; and prepare it in this way, except that you sieve it with egg white; and place its mixture not thick, nor thin. Then use it.
Another method of plastering (bandage), for setting a broken bone, and reducing a dislocation, and twisting. Take almess, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mugath: and steep all of them in ten parts of myrrh, and five parts of both aloes, and twenty parts of alethil: grind everything, sift it, and mix with the water of alethil; or with sheep's milk whey if the bandage is heated for a sick person. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, it quickly sets broken bones, and benefits many people.
Another method of plastering (bandage), which also benefits a fracture and twisting. Take mugath, mess, white alteae, all in ten parts of myrrh, and five parts of both aloes, six parts of Armenian pitch in twenty parts: grind everything well, sift it, and make with water or sheep's milk whey: and administer.
Another method of plastering (bandage) for joints and bones separated from their places: it relieves the pain that occurs to them, and stops the splitting of the bone, and the break. Take raw wool, immerse it in vinegar, and boil it in oil: place it over the site.
And indeed, there is no virtue of restoration in this plaster, but it is good for the treatment of a hot abscess: and it expels pains properly.
Another method of plastering (bandage), which restores a broken bone. Take leaves of the deaf fig, and leaves of wild papaver: grind everything, and make a plaster from both.
Another shortened method of plastering (bandage), used for the restoration of a broken bone: and when you wish for the resolution of the remaining abscess. Make a mixture of the root of althea, chamomile, marshmallow flowers, and barley flour, taking equal parts of each: grind all and make it with wine, unless the member is already heated.
If it is already heated, then use that mixture with coriander water or just water.
A stronger method of plaster for resolving a hard abscess during the restoration of a member. Make a mixture of the root of althea, linseed, beechwood, medick, samsuc, marshmallow flowers, chamomile, taking equal parts of each: grind all and make it with willow water, or sweet water, or wine. Apply the mixture to the member according to its warmth and the calmness of the heat.
However, Hippocrates did not say in his book that it should be placed on a broken member during its restoration, except for a plaster made from beeswax and oil: nothing else; and he said it should be of a medium consistency between thickness and subtlety.
Galen, however, seemed to think that something should be placed on a broken member during its restoration which has the ability to draw out heat, such as myrrh, aloe and frankincense, and similar substances from what we have mentioned.
A method of plaster for treating injuries and pain. Make a plaster from mugath, chickpeas, cut human hair or bird feathers, althea, and salt in equal parts: grind them all into powder, and make the plaster out of it.
The amount of plaster that should be used, so that the bandage remains in place, is as follows: you should then loosen it if there is no persistent pain, itching, or the bone moves from its place: then do not loosen it for several days. If it happens to him at the place that there is violent itching, or troubling pain, or swelling: then hurry and dissolve it in an hour himself, and remove the plaster from him: and take soft cloth, or sea sponge, moist: and submerge it in warm water, and wash with it the place until the itching subsides, and the pain sits still.
If it happens to him at the place that there is violent itching, or troubling pain, or swelling: then hurry and dissolve it in an hour himself, and remove the plaster from him: and take soft cloth, or sea sponge, moist: and submerge it in warm water, and wash with it the place until the itching subsides, and the pain sits still. Then allow the limb to rest for an hour. Then place upon him a piece of flax soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind over it at night until you are sure of the disappearance of the hot inflammation, and the swelling rests, and the pain is removed from it.
Then proceed with a subtle bind and a small plaster: do not bind it tightly at first with your tight bind, but gently with the thin one until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling, and all other symptoms have been completely removed, and you need a plaster and bind, then proceed with it as you did at first equally.
If nothing of what we mentioned happens to the limb, then do not dissolve it before three, four, five or seven days. And when it is discharged after twenty days, according to what appears to you from the dissolution of the limb, as we said, until when there remains a fracture and adherence of coagulated flesh on it: add also in the bind more than your first bind, and also in the thickening of food, according to what was mentioned before its remembrance.
But if you see the place of fracture already to dry out and become thinner than necessary: then know that nutrition is prevented from reaching it. Boil the ointment with warm water in every place where you dissolve it, and let it be for three days, and loosen slightly the stricture (or narrow passage).
By this procedure, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
What foolish restorers do instead of a limb fracture, when it is not restored as it should be at first, and it is restored according to its curvature, is an error due to their operation, and there is great fear: And if it were correct, the ancients would have said so in their books, and they would have operated with it.
And I have not seen anywhere the trace of such a thing, and it is more correct that it should not be done.
Chapter on making an incision in the head.
Types of fractures are diverse, and their shapes numerous; their causes are distinguished.
For from a fracture is that which occurs by striking with an edge: and striking removes the bone entirely to the extent it reaches the siphac (a soft tissue beneath the bone), just as an ax does in wood; and for this reason, this type of fracture is called Axial.
Or the edge of a sword cuts some bone and only cuts its surface, but does not penetrate through the incision to the end; and this type of fracture is called Calcani, that is, Absolute Lever.
And there are two types of wounds in fractures, either large or small.
And from a fracture is that which is crushing or grinding; and its cause is striking with a stone, or falling on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also penetrating, which already approaches the panniculus (a fatty tissue beneath the skin), which is under the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone: and the wound of these two types of fractures is also either small or large.
And from a fracture, which is hidden within the bone in subtlety like hair, is a small split, and because of this it is called this kind of fracture Capillary.
And from these (kinds of fractures) is a fracture that happens by accident or striking with a stone, and those like them that make the bone plate to penetrate into the inside, and it becomes concave at that place: just as it happens to the caldarii (metalworkers) of iron, when they are struck and part of them penetrates into the inside.
Most certainly, this happens in moist skull bones, such as those of children.
All these kinds of fractures are made with sawing and separation, and I will come back to the subject in its place.
We recognize all these kinds of fractures by examination over it, and investigation of them with tests, and removal of the corrupted flesh that is over them.
The Capillary kind is recognized by this, that it shows on the bone, is scraped off, and a thin layer is laid over it with pitch: for the fracture appears black.
In treating a fracture, first consider the condition of the sick person: if you see from the conditions that it indicates a clear sign of fear, such as cholera vomiting, extension, loss of intellect, absence of voice, fainting, acute fever, discharge of eyes, redness of them and similar things from other conditions in various dispositions, there is certainly no recovery: and if you see the conditions not frightening to you, and hope for health, then begin its treatment.
If someone comes to you wounded in the first place when he is wounded: and it is in winter days, then it is necessary that you study in the removal of the bone before the 14th day according to all dispositions.
If it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before it becomes corrupt beneath the bone from the fat, and such accidents happen as we have described.
If the fracture of the bone has already reached the fat touching the brain and is accompanied by fragments and traction: then it is necessary that you cut the broken and smoothed part, as I have told you.
And it is to be shaved the head of the wounded: and dig on top of the bone as much as possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light on what is weak.
If it happens to you during the digging of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess: then oppose it with what is necessary. And it is to fill the place with rags soaked in wine and rose oil: and let the wound rest until the abscess settles down, and be safe from the flow of blood. Then begin in the treatment of the bone, and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
The first method is to cut the bone with a knife having a narrow blade: and this is its shape.
Then administer another knife, narrower still: and this is its shape.
Then administer yet another knife, wider still.
The second method is that there should be with you a number of different knives, some of which are wider than others, and some of which are shorter than others: and they should have their edges at the extremities sharpened, and made of steel or iron that has been hardened: and use them with ease in striking the knife, so as not to concuss the head and injure it.
If the bone is hard and firm: then it is necessary that you bore around its circumference before you administer Incisoria, with Terebris which are named Terebra not penetrating deeply; they are not called so because they do not pass beyond the bone's marrow to what is behind it, since the tip of the Terebra is rounded above what is under its head and sharp, like an edge, and a small circle prevents it from being submerged and passing through the bone's thickness.
It is necessary that you take a large number of Terebris from these, one for each thickness of the bone, until there is Terebra for every part of the skull; whose sharp tip's length and width should be according to the thickness of the said skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of Terebrums or Trepans.
The quality of boring around the broken bone is: that you place a Terebra on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know the bone has been penetrated. Then change the Terebra to another place, and place what is between every hole of a size corresponding to the thickness of the radius or approximately that.
Then cut with the Incisoria between every hole from the bone: and make it thinner than possible above that from the edge of softness, as we said, until you extract the bone, either with your hand or with some other tools which you have prepared for this purpose: such as tenaliae, and subtle forpices.
And it is necessary that you be careful to avoid with the greatest care that the Terebra or the Incisoria touch any part of the siphac [a type of horn used for surgical instruments].
When you are boring around the bone and separating it from the siphac [horn], if it is continuous with it and you extract it, it is necessary that you smooth and even its remaining part on the bone with another similar tool, like an Incisoria. It is necessary that it [the instrument] should be thin and subtle more than other cutting instruments.
If something remains of small bones or cartilage: then take it with ease, along with what you have at hand from your instruments. Then, care for the wound afterwards with lints and the ointments that I mentioned.
Another method of operation is easy, completely free from fear: which Galen said and praised greatly: and this is his saying. It is necessary for you first to begin by uncovering the bone fragment from the place where the fracture is more violent and visible: until when you have uncovered that part and placed under it the end of this curved cutter, which has this form.
The curved part of the cutter does not cut anything: and the sharp end of it on both sides going along its length, as you see: so that the curved part may be fixed to the handle, its sharp edge falling on the bone.
Then strike on the cutter from one side with a small hammer until you have cut through the whole bone easily, as it is turned: and be careful that the cloth does not slip off what happens accidentally, which you should fear completely.
And if the physician were not more foolish than men, and less ignorant of it, nor asleep, then if something remained attached to the bone from the cloth in certain places, he would cut it off with the end of the curved cutter and free it easily. It is freed from it without harm and without fear.
If the bone fracture does not penetrate to the cloth, and what is cut off from the bone is its face, and roughness and subtle cartilage remain in it: then it is necessary that you scrape away the roughness, and excavate the cartilages with sharp scissors of various quantities which you have prepared, so that it may be possible for you to apply them more appropriately in every place according to what the operation leads you, and the fractured bone, and its figure. It is necessary that you administer [the operation] according to your reason, firstly by sawing the bone, more widely with these retractors. Then use a finer instrument after that.
It is necessary that you administer [the operation] according to your reason, firstly by sawing the bone, more widely with these retractors. Then use a finer instrument after that. Do not cease to do it in continuity until you reach administering [it] thinner and finer of all of them.
In every small remaining crack or fine fracture, it is necessary that you apply care, according to what leads to its correction.
And there is a matter that is not hidden upon all [things], to which any study in this art stands, and it has stood over what we have written and shown in large fractures.
If a piece of flesh is detected and retained when you are removing bone: then it is necessary that you take linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another double or triple piece of linen cloth, immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it on the first one; do what you can easily over it, so as not to burden the wound.
Then administer a wide ligature over it; do not tie it, unless to the size that holds the wound only; and release it with this care for one day or two days until you are secure from warm inflammation. Then dissolve it [the ligation], and administer after that medications with which there is excessive dryness; such as the roots of garlic, and grain of herb, and frankincense powder, and aristolochia, and similar ones; and it is to make a powder from these medications, which will be applied to the wound like those [medications] that are dry.
And universally administer all medication in it of the property which is to purify and not corrode [the wound]. And be careful according to your virtue, that the wound in your treatment is clean: neither let there be dirt of ointment, nor dirtiness.
And be careful according to your virtue, that the wound in your treatment is clean: neither let there be dirt of ointment, nor dirtiness. Do not allow pus to accumulate on it at all: because when pus gathers upon the brain tissue, it corrupts it and makes it putrefy, and afterward great harm occurs to the sick person after that; and already it has happened on the scalp of the head, when the bone is cleansed by him, especially when the care for it is neglected, a blackness appears on its surface.
Therefore, if the accidents that I have mentioned occur to the sick person, know that it is certainly dangerous (or fatal).
And if the blackness does not occur except from the medicine placed on him and it is in the virtue of that medicine to make it black: then you should take one part from honey and three parts from rose oil, and anoint it with them. Then place it on the scalp; afterward take care of it with appropriate remedies for healing until it is healed.
Chapter on the restoration of the nose when broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except for two of its upper parts simultaneously, or one of them: because they are two bones; since below it is cartilaginous, it does not break, and harm such as attrition, twisting, or swelling does not occur to it unless there is attrition, twisting, or inflammation.
If one of the two parts is broken, then you should insert a small finger into the nostril and align the fracture with your index and thumb fingers from outside until the nose returns to its normal shape: and do this skillfully, easily. And be careful that no pain occurs to the sick person through your operation.
If a fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and it cannot be reached by a finger: then, it should be adjusted at the end of the radius, where there is a small swelling.
If [the injury] happens to be in two parts, then make it similar [to the other side]: and advance with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, at a place quiet for cauteries of warm water.
Then insert a piece of flax from linen cloth into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part: or insert two pieces if the fracture is in two parts: and there should be a bulge of such size on the flax that it fills the nostril opening.
And some of the ancients who were restorers said that flax should be soaked in butter and changed every day. But I do not see this [method] as good, rather it should be soaked in egg white made with flour mill powder. Then release the flax when the bone has hardened and the cartilage has become hard.
Sometimes a feather of a goose is inserted into the nose in place of the flax, after soft cloth has been placed over it: and this holds the nasal fracture more firmly, and prevents the weak from being prevented from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you want, do it: if you don't want, do it with flax.
If the nose again develops a cautery while working on an abscess, pack the nose with linen soaked in tar, or with pitch infused in rose oil, or with a little turpentine.
But if it does not develop a hot cautery, then the nose should be packed with flour from sesame seeds and frankincense powder mixed with egg white. Then place a soft cloth on top of it (the wound), and do not tie the nose with anything at all.
If however the nose bones are broken in a small fracture or ground down, then it is necessary that you make an incision over them and extract them with instruments suitable for the purpose. Then either make an incision and take care of it with the one that coagulates and consolidates with plasters suitable for that purpose, or with ointments.
If it should happen that a wound occurs between the nose, then it is necessary to treat it with linen cloths, and to administer a lead cannula until healed.
Chapter on the restoration of the lower jaw when broken.
When the lower jaw is disrupted and the fracture is not with a wound: then observe whether its fracture is only outside and it does not break into two and bends towards the interior: then its recognition is easy.
If the fracture is on the right side of the bone, you must insert your left index finger into the diseased jaw: Similarly, if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert your right index finger: And push the swelling of the fracture gently towards the outside with your other hand properly from the inside part of the bone and with it, equalize it.
If the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two parts, then it is necessary to apply traction from both sides according to correctness until its alignment is possible.
But if already there is movement or disruption of the teeth, then bind those which you hope remain with a thread of gold, silver or silk. Then place on the broken jaw a piece of raw hide. Then place over it a double cloth and put on the large cross made well over it or an equal piece of shoe leather, in length of the jawbone.
Then bind it above as your binding for it is ready and its application is suitable until it does not break: And instruct the sick man to rest calmly and quietly: And put his food in mild broths.
If you estimate that something has changed in its shape in some way: Then hasten and cut it open on the third day. Then adjust what has changed from it, and cover it with plaster from milled flour mixed with egg white or wheat flour, after you have removed the raw hide: And put over the plaster a soft wool.
While that adhesive plaster remains on it, and the bone arrangement does not change, leave it be without removing it until it heals; or bind the fracture. This fracture is often bound for three weeks.
If a warm boil occurs in the meantime, then administer what I have said repeatedly for its reduction until that boil is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider it. If a piece of bone is already separated from the bone or from a tendon, then be subtle about it, to remove those pieces of tendon along with whatever suits you for their removal from the instruments.
If the wound opening is narrow, then enlarge it with a leech according to your need.
Then when you have removed the tendon pieces, and there remains nothing of them, or if the wound opening is wide: and if not, place on it ointments that suit for that, and let it heal until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of the Fork (when it breaks).
The fork is broken most frequently from the anterior part of the shoulder, and its breakage occurs in one of three modes.
Or it breaks into two parts, with tendons possibly involved: and this is easier for its restoration.
Or it happens so that there are pieces of bone involved: and this is more difficult to restore.
Or it is a break with a wound.
And the operation in this case when the break is without a wound is that you have two assistants present, one holds the limb with the broken fork, and the other extends the neck toward the opposite side.
Then you should align the broken part with your fingers until its shape is correct; and there should be neither a projection nor a hollow.
If you require more extension, then it is necessary that you place under the patient's wound sphere made of cloth or wool; and let its size be according to your need; stretch and elevate the lever, and compress the sphere with your hand until it equals the fracture, as needed.
If you cannot pull the extremity of the lever outside: because it has reached deep: then it is necessary that the patient lies on his neck; and place under his shoulder a saw medium in size, and press the minister of his shoulder towards the lower part, until the bone of the lever that is in the depth rises upwards: and then correct the fracture, and make it even with your fingers.
If however you feel a splinter from the lever and it moves: then it is necessary that you probe over it, and remove the splinter with ease.
If however it is retained in the bone, then apply an instrument of incision that you have prepared for this purpose, after you place under the lever a tool which saves silk: and the tool is made of wood or iron. And this is its shape.
It resembles a snail shell without a hollow.
And let its width be according to the bone you need it for and its size. The length of it also, as far as possible for your operation.
And have two ends, as you see: and one end should be broad, and the other narrow.
If however the wound which you have cut when you remove the splinter of bone is wide and gaping, and sews itself with hot cautery: then join together two lips of the wound with stitching.
And if the wound is small or you fear hot cautery: then fill the wound with cloths and wool according to the size of the wound cut.
If a hot abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it on top of it.
However, if the bone is constricted without a wound and without incision, it should be covered with a plaster made from milled flour with egg white. And place soft wool on top of it. Then put a small ball under its pulsation, if needed. Then take a bloodletting instrument, that is the rotula which the Saracens hold, which should be very long; and its width should be one palm or approximately that. Then place a folded piece of wool over the soft wool and the plaster. Then take an awl from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then fold it into the cloth. Then fold the cloth with the awl into the place of the incision over the fracture and the site. Then apply the incision to the fracture as it is described, and fold it over the neck of the healthy side and the weak side, and under the pulsation of the healthy side and the sick side: and bring it over the entire part, so that it is clear to you how the constriction holds the fracture correctly: and this should not be hidden from you, and all of it, so that the awl does not remove the fractured bone. And for this reason, it is necessary to examine the sick side every day. When does the ligament soften and you see that the awl has already been removed? Then adjust it, and tighten the ligament.
Then place the sick person's sleep on his back, and put a small pillow under its pulsation near its sleep at night, so that when it rises, help comes to its side. Lift therefore the fracture of the clavicle by lifting the humerus.
Tie the arm to his neck; do not loosen the bandage unless it happens due to an incident caused by itching or abscess, for twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see his face towards that (the affected area). And add to the constriction, and let it be, until it is restored and hardened.
The fracture of the clavicle is bound and supported as much as possible for twenty-eight days; sometimes it heals more quickly in some people.
Chapter on the restoration of the fracture of the spatula (scapula).
The spatula is seldom broken, and at its wide end: and it is not broken from anything except its edges.
When it is broken from that place, and it is broken in the middle of it, and it is not recognized except by touch, then according to its figure prepare the treatment for it. And reduce it to its natural form by any means of your intellect that is possible for you.
Then place upon it powder of millstone with egg white, and soft starch, and place on top a small piece of double cloth. Then place upon it a broad awl from thin wood according to the quantity of the spatula or larger by little.
If however there is a depression under the awl at any part of the spatula, then fill that depression with soft starch until the awl descends according to its equal level.
Then bind on top with long alum thread in an appropriate constriction and the firmness of the awl until it is not moved from its place.
And consider the binding every day. Whenever therefore the binding becomes soft, bind it again, and if the awl is moved from its place, let it lie weak on its healthy side.
And the spatula certainly recovers in twenty days or forty days.
When this number is complete, then loosen the binding: and you will be secure. For it is made from bones whose breakage is not feared nor their solution.
If however a fragment of bone protrudes and pierces under the skin, then cut through it, and remove it. And do as we said in the fracture of a collarbone from cauterization by hot metal, if something happens from it.
§ On the restoration of a breast fracture.
Chest occasionally breaks in the middle of itself, and it happens rarely. Its extremities indeed break more often than the chest itself does, due to its accidents.
When the middle of it breaks down and tends towards the lower parts, it causes a very strong pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, perhaps vomiting blood, and a hollow appears in the fractured bone that is not concealed to the sense; The restoration of this is to lay the weak one on his back, and place between his shoulders a poultice.
Then press his shoulders, and apply the ribs from both sides or flanks with hands; Subtly cut in the equator of it, according to all possible ways that this can be done and fit, until the figure of the bone returns, as required.
Then place on it an emplaster and a plaster, and place above it a small star from a thin wood, such as willow or chalice, and others like them, after you fold it in a cloth. Then subtly tie it on the broken bone so that it is not removed; and go around with the bandage according to its roundness to the back several times, and bind it tightly with a suitable binding.
Then consider the bandage in every hour, and whenever it becomes soft, tighten it; And if necessity of its dissolution arises due to itching or corrosion that happens at the place, or pain, or abscess: then hurry, and dissolve it, remove the poultice, and correct what happened with the treatment.
Then return the poultice if you see it towards that face; And attach a binding to it until it is healed.
Chapter on Restoration of Ribs when they break.
You should know that the fracture of a costa does not occur except in thick places following the back, and from their extremities starting from the front side. And it does not happen to them by rubbing, unless for this reason: because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of such a thing is not hidden from sensation during examination with fingers.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that the fracture is adjusted with fingers according to the possible method, until the shape is made even, as required. Then apply plasters, and bind the broken bone with a splint if it requires one.
If the fracture of a costa is towards the interior: then a severe pain occurs in the weak, and puncturing, just as puncturing that which happens to one who has pleurisy; because the bone pierces the membrane, and also difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood much: and this is difficult for treatment.
And the ancients were skilled in this with many devices; and among them are those who say that you should place food for the weak which generates inflammation and wind, so that the stomach is inflamed, extended, and pushed outwards to move the fracture towards the outside.
We rather avoid causing a quick cause for making a hot abscess if it does not occur. But if it has already occurred, then you can add it to that.
And some of them said: place a windy substance on the spot, then press it strongly, and it is more like reasoning. However, it is feared that a windy substance will attract excess to the spot due to the weakness of the arrangement.
And some of them said: the place should be covered with wool already soaked in hot oil; and lice should be placed between the ribs until it is filled up; so that the binding will be equal when you pull it off according to roundness.
Then the sick one is treated for pleurisy with food and medicine.
If the affliction presses hard upon the sick one and it cannot be borne, and if we prick the wound with a lancet, causing pain, and we fear for the sick one: then it is necessary that we make an incision above the affected place, and expose the broken rib. Then we shall place upon it an instrument which serves as a saw, whose recollection has been mentioned earlier: and we shall cut the bone easily, and we shall cut it itself.
Then we shall bring together two lips of the wound, if it is large with stitches: and we shall heal it with plasters until it heals.
If an abscess occurs in the meantime which is hot, then hurry, and pour powdered pig's bristles into sweet oil, and place it over the spot: and the sick one shall be treated with it as appropriate for an abscess.
In the meantime also let him lie upon his side on that side which he sleeps, until it is healed.
Chapter on the restoration of a fracture of the spine and neck.
Fracture sometimes occurs in the bones of the neck, and it rarely happens that they rub against each other; and similarly for the vertebrae of the back. If such a thing happens to anyone, and you wish to know if it will heal or not: then consider. For if you see his hands already softened, stunned, and dead; and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them; and when you press them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, nor does he feel pain in them: then you will know that it will not heal, and in more than one condition it is mortal.
If he moves them and feels the pressure with his fingers and the prick: then you will know that the neck bone is safe, and that the broken one will heal with treatment.
If such a thing happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know if it will heal or not: then consider his feet; then if you see that they are softened and the same thing occurs in them as in the hands: and then when he lies on his back, wind comes out without will; and when he lies on his stomach, wind comes out without will; and when he lies on his back and wishes to urinate, but cannot: then you will know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labor in the treatment of it.
But if nothing similar happens to him, it is less severe.
And the treatment for such a thing that happens to him is, you should apply hot oil to the rubbed vertebra; so that you place hot oil on the softened vertebra alone; or with sheep wool soaked in vinegar.
Place it on the same thing several times a day, until when the hot abscess is quieted: then place one of the strengthening and drying plasters on the location, and bind it tightly with a bandage; and tell the sick person to rest quietly and peacefully. He should not sleep over a part where he found pain, until it is healed.
If it happens by the fracture of the bone, or something that is separated from it: then it is necessary that you lay on the skin, and remove that. Then join together two lips of the wound, if it is large, with suture. Then take care of himself with some ointment, until healed.
If the last bone of the ulna is broken and it is the end of the shin: then it is necessary to insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and equal the fractured bone with the other hand, as much as possible, and by second adjustment.
Then place him on it, and remove it: and take care of the wound with the treatment that preceded, until healed.
Chapter on the repair of the anus.
Chapter XI.
Rarely do the bones of the ankles break. If they break, then the fracture of these bones is not anything other than a comminution at their ends; and they are cut through lengthwise, and they bend towards the inner parts; and there occurs pain in that place, and puncture; and the leg that breaks becomes numb.
And the repair of it is that you extend your hand over it until you are standing over the fracture in its figure.
If the fracture is only at their ends, then equalize that fracture, as much as possible, according to your preparation, until it becomes similar in its natural figure.
If the fracture is through length or already bending towards inner parts: then make the weak lay on his stomach until the preparation for repairing that fracture is ready. When you equalize it, place ointment on it; then place a splint of wood or hide over it; and tighten the bandage above it, so as not to fear movement of the fracture, nor removal of the splint.
And let the cavities [of the body] be equally concave with what they contain, until they are seized by a ligature in accordance with their equality; and command the sick person to sleep on his back or on his healthy side.
If, however, an abscess becomes hot: then cease from extending it and its healing until the hot abscess is seated; and place on him what sits upon it as previously, then return to its healing; and ligature it as needed.
If, however, it should happen that a fracture occurs in the bone, or if it is crushed, extract it.
Chapter on the healing of fractures. Aid is given.
The aid is that which is between the elbow and the head of the spatula [tool].
If it should break, then its healing is according to one of two methods.
One method is that you take a flat board of medium thickness, in the following shape:
And two ligaments shall be tied at both ends.
Then it is suspended from a raised place, and let the sick man sit on a seat. Then cast his broken arm upon a log until it reaches the notch of the same log, where the break of the log is attached.
Then place something heavy above him to stretch him towards below. Then the physician sets the break with his hands until it returns, as is required.
Another method is that the sick man lies down on his neck and suspends his hand from his neck with a rope. Then order two servants to hold one above the break with their hands and let another hold below him and stretch each of them in turn.
And if you wish for the extension to be stronger, then bind under the break with a rope and above it with another; let each of the servants stretch in turn towards their respective sides.
If the break is near the shoulder joint, then it should be bound under the notch and another above towards the elbow.
And similarly if the break is near the elbow joint: then it should be bound above that place and above the elbow itself.
Then set the break easily without exertion, until it is aligned as required and a good dressing is applied; then bind it if no hot abscess occurs.
If a hot abscess occurs, then refrain from binding until the seventh day. Place a linen cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil on him until the abscess subsides; then bind it and the method of binding is to place an ointment over the break.
Then lay a new cloth over the ointment. Then gather the arm upon the same support, and place his open hand upon his own shoulder.
Place the cloth and bind the arm to the support, so that the arm remains in place like a star; if it does not prevent you from doing anything or if nothing alters on you due to a broken bone.
If you fear that something may change due to a broken bone, then use stars; and there is one which you place over the fracture itself, which is broader and stronger than other stars. And place the width of every star at the length of a finger; and let the length of stars, with an addition of three fingers, be from all sides regarding the fracture.
Then bind on the stars as I said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is to be so that your binding is closer to the place of fracture. As the fracture extends more, the binding shall be less.
If you see the position of stars and binding, as we have said, at the hour when you restore a limb immediately, then do so; and if you fear a hot abscess, then release the binding and stars until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the binding in all three days, so that itchiness, swelling or food does not reach the limb due to the surplus of binding. Therefore correct this matter as we have said.
If however you are confident that nothing will happen from it; then do not release the binding, unless after three days or more.
And let the sick person lie on his back and place his hand on his stomach; and place under the support a wool blanket full of wool evenly.
And consider at all hours of the night or day that the shape of the broken limb does not loosen and that the binding is not softened. Correct this altogether with your power.
Place the food for the sick according to the order which we have prescribed, so that it is a subtle food first, until the bone begins to be bound; then it is necessary that you increase its size.
For the custom of assistance and the leg is that it should be bound for forty days; then it is necessary that it is loosened, a bath is given, and it is treated with ointments which correct this.
If the fracture is foul, scrape it; then do not loosen from it the bandage and pegs for fifty days, and up to two months.
On the restoration of the fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two forearm bones.
One of them is small; and that is the one which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is the one which is placed below the small one from the lower part.
Perhaps only the larger forearm bone is broken, or perhaps both are broken.
If the smaller forearm bone above is broken, then the restoration of this is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower forearm bone is broken, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And this is worse when both bones are broken simultaneously.
If the bone that is broken is the smaller forearm bone above, then it is fitting for the doctor in its restoration, to place an extension smaller with ease until it is equal to it.
If however the larger forearm bone is the one that is broken: then it is necessary to place a strong extension.
And it is necessary to place the shape of the hand at its restoration, and its extension extended above the pulley. The thumb of the hand should be higher than all the fingers; and the inferior joint should be higher than all the fingers or remaining [parts]; and if the patient is weak and sitting, the cubit bone should be square with respect to itself, and the pulley should be in front of it or directly above it at an appropriate height so that the weak patient does not suffer labor.
Then let the attendant extend the arm from its lower part, either with his hand, or with a rope. And another attendant shall extend it likewise from above.
Then the physician shall equalize the bone until he can reduce it to its best possible shape.
If there are shards in a bone fracture, then try to reduce every fragment into its proper place with your strength.
If it appears that a shard is separated and pierces the skin, and there is no hope for its restoration: then fill it over with material as we have mentioned before.
If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have made a special chapter for that: therefore receive its treatment from there.
If a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your treatment, then prepare linen cloth with wax made from rose oil, and white beeswax, and its consistency should be between thickness and fineness; then wrap the linen cloth around it tightly with a thin string until the abscess subsides; then remove the wax, and apply an ointment prepared from flour mill powder and egg white. Then place a splint, and let the splint placed over the fracture be slightly wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in various arrangements; whether it is a fracture in one splint or two splints together.
Then place a stronger and firmer strap over the location of the fracture, and apply it somewhat more to the upper or lower part of the fracture; or place a softer strap slightly below the one mentioned earlier, according to what was remembered in the beginning of the chapter.
The fabric that is wrapped around a fracture should be soft, wet and not hard or rough. The thread with which it is stitched should be made from flax, neither too fine nor too coarse, as we have said.
Consider the limb and the ligament after days. If something happens requiring correction, such as an itch on the limb: then it is necessary that you apply warm embrocation to the limb with hot water until the itch subsides, and leave the limb unbound until it subsides, then repeat binding.
If the binding has already been softened and the bone already removed, and similar cases: then you should correct it with your power, and consider again.
If nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excess binding, then it is necessary to soften it a little and leave it for some days until it goes towards nourishment, then bind it again.
But if nothing of those things that were mentioned occurs in the sick one, then it is not necessary to loosen it, unless after twenty days or about this much.
Then the hand of the sick man is suspended at his neck. And let his neck be kept straight and guarded with your power against violent movements. Make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored within thirty or thirty two days; it may perhaps be restored within twenty eight days. All this is according to the dispositions of the constitutions and the dispositions of virtue.
Chapter on the Restoration of Fractures: Hand.
Know that the break in the hand by combing and knots of fingers is rare. It does not happen to him except through excessive rubbing.
When a fracture or abrasion of the foot occurs, then it is necessary that the sick person sits on a level seat next to him. Then place his hand upon it extended (the injured part).
Then let the attendant spread out the broken bones, and let the physician align them, until they join together well.
Then it is necessary to place plaster and wool (linen), if no hot abscess occurs. Then place a splint on top according to the size of the location, and you have already wrapped it with linen cloth.
If the fracture is near the heel of the foot, then make something like a ball from cloth: and tell the sick person to tread on it with his injured foot: then tie it up with long linen cloth.
And let the splint be made of soft leather, with which to apply it to the skin of the foot: and tie it on as necessary.
If the fracture is at the outer side (of the foot), then it is necessary to place one splint on top and another one underneath the foot, and let the hand be open straight. Then place a support, as the hand is rolled (into a tube): and extend it between the fingers with a ligation.
If there should be a fracture in any of the knuckles (of the fingers), if it is the thumb, then let it be aligned as necessary. Then tie it to the sole of the foot (the injured hand): and if it is a finger, place a straight splint under it, smaller than usual, so that the fracture may be elevated and not moved.
If the fracture is in the remaining fingers (such as the middle finger, index, little finger or ring finger), then let it be aligned and tied to the healthy digit that follows it, or tie them all together following their order: this is better. Place a small pin over it (the wound), as we said on the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the arrangement of the incision of hot cautery: and oppose it with what is necessary, when something happens from that, according to its repetition of his narrative.
Chapter on the treatment of a broken hip.
The thighbone (Femur) often breaks and becomes noticeable to the senses because it turns towards the inner and posterior parts.
The restoration of it (the thighbone) is done in such a way that a ligament is tightened above the fracture and another ligament below it. And the weak one (patient) stretches himself over his face.
Then, stretch out every ligament that serves for its side according to equality; and this when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If, however, it is a fracture near the root of the hip (acetabulum), then let there be a ligament towards the side of the ilium, so that the extension falls upward; and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if it is a fracture near the knee, then let there be a ligament near the knee so that the extension falls towards the lower parts.
Then the physician straightens the bone with both his hands until he returns it to its natural shape; and joins the bone by a good union.
Then it is necessary that you place an ointment and a bandage, if no abscess appears on the limb. If an abscess occurs there (on the limb): then leave it for some days until the abscess becomes warm, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage of this should be in such a way. It is necessary that you wind a hard linen (or bandage) over the fracture; and fold it twice or thrice, so that there remains an excess of it. Then fold the leg (or thigh), until the heel reaches the root of the toes.
Insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee, and let the ends of the thread come out from the lower parts (of the two parts). Then roll up the leg and thigh what remains over from the bandage.
Then place a small star on the hip at the site of the break, and place another star on the bone of the leg.
Then fill the gaps between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that it is bound tightly.
Begin (the bandage) strongly from the middle of the break in three or four turns. And as you extend (the bandage) away from the site of the break with the rope, let your bandage be looser and softer, and more gentle.
Then direct (the ends of) the thread that you inserted between the hip and the leg, towards the two ends. Tie three stars on each end. Then go with the two ends of the thread to the lower parts, until you reach the ankle of the foot. And tighten (the bandage) also with the ends of the stars on both sides, so that the bandage is not moved from its place.
Then let the bandage remain there until the bone is healed, so that no rot is caused to the limb, no abscess, no swelling, and similar things happen. But if something happens from that (the bandage). Approach it (the problem) then with the solution of it, and correct everything that happens from there completely, as we have said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone sticking out from the broken bone: then it is necessary that you cut it off, if it is possible for you. And if not (you can remove the bone piece with a cut), then place a bandage on it, and take care of the one who preceded the reminder, until it heals.
And sometimes this hip fracture is tightened, even when additional studs are added, as we said in the aid and in the arm.
However, in the treatment of this broken hip, it does not occur that the lame limp. And if it is restored alone, except that another leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that the one whose it is always limps.
And know that the hip tightens within fifty days, or is increased too little, or decreased too little; all of this is according to the diversity of temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter on the restoration of a knee joint fracture.
Know that the knee joint rarely breaks, but it often wears down (or wears away).
But if it occurs that it is broken, then it is not other than a split or a hole in its parts. It is with a wound and without a wound. And it stands on top of the whole thing with feeling.
And the restoration of it is thus: It is made equal to what was broken from its parts with fingers until it is gathered together and joined according to what is possible through equality, ease, and skillful craftsmanship.
Then apply a plaster from wheat flour or milled wheat, with egg white; and place over it a round stud, if needed; and tie on top with a binding appropriate for that.
Then consider all the conditions that we have mentioned in other fractures; such as hot abscess and similar things; so that you are ready for any occurrence with what is appropriate for it until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that there are two bones in the leg, of which one is large and called by the name Cruris; and the other is subtle, and called Focile.
It happens to both kinds of fractures that it happens to two bones in the arm: and because of this, restoration is made of it, just as the restoration of an arm equally, and the operation is one.
If two bones are broken simultaneously, the leg is converted to all parts.
And if a thin bone is broken, the leg is converted to the anterior part.
And if a thick bone is broken, you find it lower: and it is from those which are not hidden to you.
Then administer extension, equalization, and ligature of the stars equally according to that.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a dirty one, having many seths: so that the extension is less, and the alluvialtion is milder on your part.
And in the leg there is something more than in the arm concerning the operation: and it is that when you equalize the stars, and complete your entire operation, then take two bands from pine wood, which is used in the making of solar panels placed under tables: and they are made from the blades of the palm: and choose among them those with a slight thickness, and neither be from the very small ones: and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then fold over one cloth with two folds according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other part on the other side: and they should be from the knee down to the foot.
Then bind the two bands in three places at both ends and in the middle. For if tightening is forbidden, the leg bends to the right and left, and stands with a good position.
And now let the crib be made of wood according to the length of the leg, and place the leg in it to keep it still.
It is greatly necessary that you do it in a fracture when there is a wound properly.
Then consider the Leg (Crura) on every day, and study with the utmost diligence from purging, or swelling, or other similar conditions. If something happens from among those (conditions), then oppose it with the appropriate means, until it is healed, if God wills it.
On the fracture of the bones of the Foot and Fingers.
A fracture does not happen entirely in the calcaneum (heel bone).
Bones of the Foot sometimes suffer a fracture, and even less so do the bones of the fingers: and it does not happen to them unless there is friction in many positions.
If a fracture happens in the bones of the Foot and you see those bones elevate over each other, then let the sick person place his foot on the ground prepared as if he is walking.
Then stand you there, and place your foot upon that which is elevated from those bones: afterwards press, or trample it; and equalize it, until it returns to its places.
Then place a plaster, and a woolen cloth on top of it: and put under the arch of the foot a small board with two flat heads.
Then bind it with an appropriate fetter when you lower it with linen cloths and other things that are required.
When three or four days have passed, then loosen the binding. For you will find the bones equalized, whether they are fractured or displaced. The operation is therefore as you see it.
If some fingers are fractured, then restore them and equalize them as I told you about the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place your finger at the length of the digit, and let it be slightly wider; then place under the foot this board that I told you, and tie it with a suitable suture.
If what is broken is from the bones and it is two, or three, or more, then place a splint of suitable size over all the fingers, rolled in linen cloth; and tie the board below the foot; and let it stand above one corner of the board outside the abdomen of the foot to hold with good retention.
It is suitable for you that you not forget to consider all the things I told you in the other fractures which I mentioned; and apply each one of them as required.
Chapter on the Fracture of a Woman's Face, and the Bone of the Chest and Man's Vertebra.
When a woman's face is fractured, then make her sit square (or upright). Then lean her towards the back of the same slightly, and let her be held backwards.
Then let an obstetrician fill the woman's wound with a linen cloth until it is filled; and let the wound on the face be shaped like a sphere.
Then the woman is shaken (or jarred), and her back is slowly raised. For that linen cloth will come out until it becomes before the door of the wound like a sphere, and the fractured bone returns (or heals).
Then place a pledget on her back. When she wants to menstruate, let her easily remove the linen cloth, then let her menstruate; and return it as much as she first inserted it; then let her return to her pledget (or pad) first.
Let her do that for seven days or approximately, for the bone itself will be restored.
And if you wish, take the bladder of an egg, and insert a reed cannula into its opening, and introduce the whole bladder into the sheath of it. Then blow through the cannula with force, until the bladder is inflated within the sheath; for otherwise it will burst.
Then fill it with a pad and let it stand for some days as we have said, until it heals.
And when the bone of the man's or woman's jaw is broken, then administer for its healing, and the same remedy that we have mentioned for the bone of Anchus.
And do not hide the rectum in close fractures, which seldom happen, especially for someone who has some knowledge or skill.
Understand my book well, for the fracture itself indicates to you in many positions the way of healing and the narrowing for its healing; understand this then.
In the case of a man's rod (penis) when it is broken, take the throat of a goose; and introduce the rod into it. Then place on top of it a covering made from cloth, and wrap it up, and let it stand for three days or about that time; and it will heal if God wishes.
Restoration of Bone Fracture When There is an Ulceration.
It is suitable for the one who has a fracture with an ulceration, especially if it is large such as the hip bone or the arm, to hasten and call a phlebotomist immediately; if the conditions of bloodletting are confirmed by you as we have said.
If the ulceration discharges blood, then it is necessary for you to hasten to its amputation so that lime powder is sprinkled over it; if there is nothing else present with you.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not delay it, unless an abscess occurs.
But if an abscess hot, then delay the restoration for the ninth day until the abscess settles. And do not approach it on the third day and the fourth day completely, for you will make bad things happen to it.
But if the broken bone is prominent under the skin, then it is necessary that you try to reduce it and bring it into alignment with your hands easily and with slight extension.
But if there is no extension possible for it and cannot be aligned by your hands, then reduce it with this instrument. And the instrument is made of iron, whose length is of seven or eight fingers, and its width according to the size of the wound. And therefore it is necessary for the physician to take three or four according to the amount required for the cure of every type of fracture in any way.
And let it be round and have a small thickness so that it does not double up when compressed on top of it during the operation hour; and let it have sharp ends, to which there should be a margin at its ends. And let the top part of it be as thick as halfway down to the bottom.
And this is the form of it.
It is necessary in this operation that the sharp end of it is bent over the protruding bone extremity; and push it together with it in one motion until when the bone returns and aligns slightly.
Therefore try to align the extremities of the fracture against each other.
If the extremities of the fracture are fine and do not accept the instrument well, then it is necessary to cut the bone's extremity until the instrument can hold it.
If you cannot reduce [the fracture] as we have described, then cut it with what is appropriate for him from the incisions which we mentioned, or saw it with one of the saws in any way possible for you.
Then file the remainder on the bone from roughness and small cortices.
Since the bone is reduced and finds a weak spot after reduction causing strong pain, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Make this. For you give great aid to the weak when you complete the restoration that you do for the bone.
Then immerse a cloth in mustard water, black. And specifically if it is summer. And do not place vinegar or anything with oil on the wound; so that it does not cause rot and decay in it.
Then administer stars (asterisks) at the completion of your bone restoration hour. And release the discovered wound, so that you cut with forceps the opening in the swellings according to the size of the wound.
Take care not to stitch the wound with the fracture. For often have foolish physicians done this; and caused death or rot, or gangrene, that is, pus, on their weak patients. And your suture should be soft, gentle, and diverse from the remaining fracture sutures.
If the wound is bad or large and you fear some of the evils we have mentioned and find pain in the place suspended, then it is not necessary that you place stars over it. And make swellings from tough cloths in the place of the stars and stitch it with them.
When however it is past one day or two and you see the wound already starting to produce pus: then remove from above the cloth which you placed over it with wine. Administer linen and unguents as per our custom to heal wounds; such as the tetrapharmacon ointment, and similar ones.
It is suitable for you to loosen the bandage. You should examine the wound every day at morning and evening until it heals and becomes well.
It is necessary to prepare the limb so that pus drains from it easily towards the lower parts.
If several days pass and the wound does not heal or does not drain pus from it: then you will know that there are fractures of small bones in that place. Therefore, you should investigate the wound with probing. How many of these fractures are separated: remove them and extract them. And those which are not separated and pierce the limb and cause pain, try to cut them off, and remove them in any way possible for you.
If an abscess or purulence occurs at the wound, i.e., if it is a boil or any other corruption and putrefaction: then you must apply to each of them what is appropriate for their treatment as described in their respective chapters.
And from the conversation in which my speech should remain, and to whom you are to give your attention when a bone is broken and protrudes from the limb, like the hip bone or shoulder blade or similar large bones: it is not presumptuous to attempt its attraction, for many times death occurs from this. Instead, leave it alone until it becomes putrid.
Perhaps it falls by itself within twenty days, or thirty.
Then care for the wound, if it seems to you: and if not, leave it alone.
Chapter on the treatment of a swelling in the step of a broken bone.
Often occurs this swelling in the step during healing of a fracture. Mainly those near joints: and it becomes foul from that member's shape. And perhaps it prevents the member from its natural function.
Therefore, consider then if the swelling is recent: administer to it medications that have astringent properties, such as aloes, frankincense, myrrh, pitch, acacia, and similar ones.
Thus, so that you receive some of these or all of them: and prepare them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and place them on the swelling on a linen cloth, and tie them firmly with a good suture.
And leave the suture, do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and put another on until the swelling is removed.
Or tie it firmly over a lead plate. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from members.
If the swelling has already turned into a stone and is tightened and urgently requires removal: then bind it from above its side, and cut off any excess protruding: or grind it with some scrapers until it is removed: and care for it until it heals.
Chapter on the treatment when the member is restored, but remains delicate afterwards, not according to its natural state.
When a bone fracture is restored and the limb remains tender and weak afterwards, it is not done except for many reasons. One of the reasons is an abundance of dissolution of ligament and its binding not as required; or due to excess tightening of ligaments, preventing nourishment from reaching the limb; or due to excessive embrocation at an incorrect time; or due to scarcity of blood in the body of the weak, and its debilitation.
The treatment for it is to feed the weak and make his body tender until blood multiplies in him. Administer a bath. Insert joy and happiness over him.
Then place pitch on the limb; so that the pitch attracts much nourishment to it. Continue embrocation of it with warm water until the nourishment heals and returns to its natural shape.
Chapter on the healing of fractured bones when they are restored twisted.
When after the restoration of a limb, twisting or projecting bone of the fracture, or nodulation occurs, and it disfigures the shape of the limb, although the limb does not prevent its natural operation; in such cases one should not listen to the speech of him who believes that the limb should be broken again.
And there have been many foolish doctors and restorers who did this on our land.
This operation is indeed highly condemnable, leading to death or great fear that it will prove fatal.
However, if the limp and nodulation are recent; then it should be embrocated with warm water in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as the leaves of althea, its root, mellilotus, and similar ones. And let it (be) an ointment like well-prepared diachylon.
Take the mucilage of the root of the marshmallow plant, grind it with chicken fat or sesame oil; and apply (it) as an ointment.
Or take the ripe figs and crush them with dove droppings and similar medications that are called those which reduce consolidation.
And sometimes a knot is loosened by frequent rubbing with an old and gentle friction, which is done with moist hands. And move the affected limb to all its parts at all hours.
If however it has already preceded (the) tortuosity and has become strong, and has turned into a stone, and the necessity of treatment with iron has been provoked: then it is necessary that it be cut above its knot, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and what exceeds from the knot or bone be cut with the thin chisels: and ease be given to it with diligence and care.
Chapter on Dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of some joints from their place. Why it (the member) should not be moved and the figure of the limb becomes deformed. And causes the occurrence of additional pain on the weakened part, and severe injuries.
Therefore when dislocation occurs, it is necessary to hasten to its restoration; nor should one delay it at all.
For if delayed, the location (joint) becomes fixed, and possibly with it the reduction of dislocation.
Therefore it is not necessary to move or stretch during the time of dislocation setting. Because it often causes spasm and painful injuries.
But when this happens, then it is necessary to hurry to the bloodletting of the weakened. Then it should be allowed to rest until the abscess subsides.
Then the limb is to be anointed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced [in size].
And all the limb shall be cured with it, when its recollection comes to its place.
I myself have already arranged the chapters on dislocation, even as to what preceded it in the fracture, from the upper part of the body down to its lower extremity.
Chapter on the care for dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two Jaws seldom get dislocated.
Dislocation occurs in two ways. Or they are moved slightly from their places and become somewhat softened. The dislocation should be reduced by complete reduction until it softens to the chest part, until the sulcus runs weak and cannot hold it, nor can it close its own mouth, and the tongue of it is wound up with speech.
When, however, the dislocation is small, then it returns to many positions by itself with scarcity.
If the dislocation is complete and whole, then it must be administered a quick reduction and not delayed too much; and there should be someone to hold the head of the sick person; and the doctor should insert his thumb into the root of the dislocation's mandible bone if the dislocation is from one part. Or he inserts both thumbs at once, if the dislocation is from two parts. And the remaining fingers of his hand should be outside, to hold them equal.
And command the sick person to clean his mandibles and to purify them for eating all parts. The doctor equalizes the mandible and pushes it back until it returns to its place.
If the reduction is difficult, especially if both mandibles are together; then administer vaporization with hot water and oil until it becomes easy to reduce both of them; do not delay the reduction of both of them at all, as we said.
When they return and are equalized, and the mouth of the sick person is closed, and they do not soften; then place a pad of linen cloths on them, with a wax made from beeswax; and rose oil.
Then bind it easily with soft bands.
And let the sleep of the sick person be on his back; and his head should be stationary between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right or left; nor should he try to chew something; instead, let his food be soft, until when the pains are removed, and the mandible is loosened; then let him eat whatever he wants, and let that be given to him easily.
Do not let him eat or drink at his mouth operation and swallowing, and talking, until the mandible is sutured and healed.
If the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some hour and do not return to their places: then fevers often occur, and there is continuous vomiting; and perhaps the weak stomach dissolves, and perhaps it vomits bile.
When you see this (scil. the difficulty of reduction): then know that it is a ruin, and he who has this often dies within ten days.
Chapter on the reduction of dislocations of the collarbone, and the extremity of the humerus.
The collarbone is not dislocated from within on the side, due to its continuity with the chest. It is sometimes dislocated towards the outside and this is perceived by touch.
The restoration is that the sick one lies on his back, and extends his arms. Then hollow out a place with your hand, for it will return there.
Then place on it the plaster and the lint, and bind it.
However, its ends following the elbow should not be dislocated unless rarely. If it is dislocated on some day: then it is necessary that it should be reduced and set in accordance with what has been said, and what is prepared for you.
Then place upon it the plaster and lint, and bandage. And command the sick one to adhere to calmness and stillness until he is healed.
With this same treatment, the shoulder joint is reduced when it is separated even from its place.
Chapter on the Reduction of Shoulder Dislocation.
Know that the shoulder is not dislocated except in three ways.
One of these is that it dislocates towards the touching part below. The second is that it dislocates towards the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated towards the upper part of the Humerus: and that happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated towards the backside, because of the spoon. And it is not dislocated towards the front side, because of the nerves.
Indeed it is dislocated and goes towards the lower side turned upside down mostly; particularly in those whose flesh is scanty. Because it comes out quickly in them and enters quickly.
But in those whose flesh is abundant, it behaves contrary to that, namely because it comes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some people that there is a blow or an accident and the humerus is dislocated by a dislocation tool when hot, and it is believed because it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary to test that, until you are certain of its nature, and then proceed with the treatment of it.
And a dislocation is known when it is towards the lower side turned upside down; and you distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy one. For you find a clear difference among them, and in the head of the humerus, there is a hollow, and beneath the swollen part of the humerus, it seems as if it is an egg; and the weak person cannot return his hand to its own ear nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards; then you find a clear swelling that is not hidden.
And this type of dislocation has an easy reduction when it is recent or the child is weak.
And its reduction is that the tool raises the hand towards the upper part. Then place your thumbs beneath it and raise the joint with strength towards its place; and the tool raises the hand and stretches it upwards. Then place her below, for he quickly returns.
If he does not return as we have said, and it is from the beginning that dislocation occurs, then the sick person should be bathed in warm water, and embrocation should be administered which softens and soothes, just as if they infuse the roots of althea, fenugreek, and melilotus in water, and it should be administered.
Then the sick one shall lie down on his back: and let there be placed beneath his chest a sphere and wool, which should be equal in softness and tightness.
Then the doctor shall place his heel on the sphere, and push the head of the humerus with force, and draw the hand of the sick one towards the lower part.
And if you wish, bring him back according to this manner: and it is that you make the man present who is taller than the sick one, and he shall stand on the side, and insert his arm under the chest of the sick one: and push the chest upwards, until the sick one is suspended in the air.
