And another servant shall draw the hand of the sick one towards the lower part of his belly. If the sick one is light: then it is necessary that he be suspended with something else, to make him heavy; for the dislocation quickly returns.
And it is brought back according to another manner: and it is that a piece of wood is shaped on the earth in the form of a round handle, like a glove maker's handle; not too heavy, nor too fine. Then let there be placed beneath the sick one, after the logs have been put on his head; and let the sick one stand according to the length of the wood.
Then extend his hand towards the lower part and extend his body from the other side with strength: for the union returns to its place quickly.
If his reduction is difficult through all that has been said, then use this care: and it is to receive a stick whose length is the measure of two arms, and its width four fingers, and its thickness two fingers: which should have a round head, so that it may be softened the entrance into the depth, according to this form.
Then wrap soft cloths around the round head, so as not to injure the weak stick.
Then place it below the weak point, so that the hand or arm may be extended over the stick to the lower part: and tie the stick to the helper and arm and end of the hand.
Then place the arm on a step of the ladder according to its width, and extend the hand towards the lower part, and let the remaining body hang suspended from the other side. For the union enters immediately.
When therefore the entrance is completed according to any method possible: then it is necessary, that you place under the weak point a sphere equal in size from wool. Then place ready ointment prepared from milled powder with olive oil and egg white on the whole shoulder, as it is rolled over.
Then tie a bandage under the weak point of suitable size: and wind the thread over the ointment above: and connect his hand to his neck and let it not be moved for seven days.
It is necessary that food be given sparingly until the member is strengthened: because it is quicker for its healing.
Then it is to be placed again after seven days or five and let it be washed with movement. If therefore it [the wound] is tightened and not softened, then it has already been healed.
If the dislocation [of a fracture] is repeatedly disturbed due to the moisture that happens to it or because of another reason: then it is necessary, that cautionery be applied to it with three holes, according to what was previously stated in the chapter on cautery.
If this is done entirely, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the dislocation is not tightened, and the plaster and the bandage are repeatedly applied and tightened above it but do not hold and cannot raise it upwards: then you know that the nerves that are at the head of the humerus have already been cut or stretched or softened; and you know that a dislocation will never heal in its place.
A dislocation that occurs towards the chest and breastbone or is reduced towards the back is made with pressure and extension using hands; until it returns and further bandaging and care are applied, until it heals.
If after healing there is heaviness in the limb and slowness of its motion, then the sick one should bathe himself many times until that heaviness is relieved, and it returns to the first nature of his limb.
Chapter on the healing of elbow dislocations.
You should know that the dislocation of the elbow occurs with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty as well; it is dislocated in all directions, especially towards the front or towards the back.
Its [the dislocation's] position is not hidden from you: because it falls under sight and touch, according to whatever form it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, its position is declared to you clearly. The joint should be joined and it cannot double the arm nor touch with it its own shoulder.
It is necessary that you rush and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a warm abscess occurs to it.
For if a warm abscess occurs to it, the reduction becomes difficult; and perhaps it does not heal at all, especially when it is toward the backside. Indeed, every kind of dislocation is worse and more painful; and death often accompanies it many times.
And the restoration of it, when it can be reduced, is to extend the hand with two hands; and the arm should be expanded, and the hands of the physician should be above the elbow and below it, and he himself should push the joint with the fingers of his hands together, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or if the dislocation is toward the frontside: then it returns in such a way that it doubles the hand with its own hand, until it strikes with the root of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not obey in reduction, then administer a strong and forceful extension; and it is that two assistants extend the arm; and hold him also, so that he is not moved during the extension.
Then turn the arm to every side when a cloth doubled long or wide bandage is wrapped around it.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he binds it with oil; so that this is from those which help in lubrication of the joint with ease. Then push the joint with a strong thrust until it returns.
After its reduction, it is necessary to place on it a plaster containing astringency and extract of egg white; and to bind it with a bandage of appropriate size. Suspend the arm of the sick person from his neck for some days.
Then release (it). If it becomes fixed in its place, then loosen the binding from it and let it be.
And if you see that the stitch is not tight enough, then reapply the plaster, the binding, and let it be for some days until it becomes tight. Then loosen (it).
If, however, it happens that there is pain after the reduction of the stitch and the condition is mobile: then administer humectation in bathing, and fine friction, and compression, until it heals, or place upon the stitch a sheep's wool fat (iliatum), that is, the greasy hide of a ram. Then bind (it) and let it be over it day and night. Then remove it, and immerse (him) in the bath. When he sweats, then gently friction the stitch. Then place upon it one, two, and three times with an alum application when entering the bath until it softens.
And if you wish, place upon it dung of a cow, moist and warm with butter, and bind (it) over it.
Do this several times. It becomes softened and returns to its normal condition.
Chapter on the cure of a dislocation by Albucasis.
Many times Almahassem's hand is dislocated, that is, the stitch between the arm and the hand.
The reduction of this dislocation is easy. On the contrary, the other joints (are more difficult). However, it is necessary that speed be in the reduction of this dislocation within the hour in which it was dislocated, before the abscess appears there; or an abscess occurs in the same place.
And the reduction of a dislocation is as follows: Place Mahassem, the weak hand, on the table, and let its attendant extend it; then the physician shall place his palm over the prominent joint, and push until it returns.
However, it must be considered if the palm has already receded towards the wrist of the hand: then let the weak hand lie flat on the table for both extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then the position of its hand should be intrinsically on the table, that is, the inner part of the hand should lie on the table, so that the physician's hand falls on top of the prominent joint.
If it returns immediately, that is good; and if not, then bind it with a plaster sitting on the abscess; and let him be left alone, do not repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate the reduction, nor can you do it again after the days have passed, but the joint remains in its twisted state; and nothing harms the weak except that it is softened by the hand, and it cannot be bound: then you will know that the nerves have been cut or rubbed.
Therefore, there is no skill in it, except that it should be tied with a cautery near the break. And perhaps it does not help with anything.
When Almahassem does not return, then place on him the plaster as we have said. Then it is bound and left for five days; afterwards it is loosened and left, and the hand is exercised.
If it loses motion and something of weight occurs there: then soften it with warm water and rubbing several times until it becomes soft.
Chapter on the treatment of finger dislocations.
When a finger is dislocated in any direction or to the back of the palm, or the palm's inside: then extend the affected finger, and push the dislocation back using your thumbs until it returns to its place.
Then hold the finger's head and turn it towards the direction of the dislocation, and let it remain like that for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it until it is equal to its straight state on that day.
When it is night, tie it as described, and do not untie it during the day; exercise it with movement, and tie it at night.
Do this for several days until it has healed.
And likewise do the same if a finger is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; tie it to that part and perform your first operation on it until it heals.
And similarly do the same when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter on the treatment of spondylolisthesis of the back.
When anyone has a vertebral spine dislocation, either complete of the neck or many vertebrae being removed from their places: then there is no cure in it, because death approaches the weak.
And the sign of it is that the sickness goes out without will, nor can he hold it; and many times some members of his soften from it, or two legs of his, or two arms of himself, or one of them; or a vertebra is removed from its place.
For many times it is removed, for many times it is the removal to four parts.
Therefore that which is removed towards the back, is called Gibbositas.
The cure of it is that you consider then, if Gibbositas occurs from childhood; then there is no cure in it, and there is no healing at all from it.
But in that which occurs by accident, or blow, or similar things, the ancient ones have already spoken about the methods of treatment in a long speech. It does not return to many when it goes forward.
I have already abbreviated from that one, whose little excuses for many because of my proximity to its intention; and in my exposition, I have shown it, and formed its instrument in a different way than they showed it and explained it.
Therefore I say that in Gibbositas which occurs from the front part, there is no healing, nor recovery from it.
And similarly in that which happens to two sides also.
And it is not cured from them, except that which occurs in the back properly, as I say; and there is, that the sick one must be spread on his face on an equal plank almost to the wall; and a wet cloth is spread under him so as not to hurt his chest.
Then place a straight stick fixed in the fire on the ground, at the end of the foot, about its head: and another stick about its feet at the other end of the foot: and let a servant hold the stick, and it not be fastened in the fire. And let another servant hold the other.
Then cover the head of the sick man, and under his chin tie a loose bandage, firm: and extend the end of the bandage to the stick which is at his head: and bind it to that.
Then tie another bandage above his ankles, and above his knees, and at his joints. Then gather all the bandages together, and bind them to the other stick which is at his feet.
Then let all the servants extend their bandages to the part of the stick, and let them not be moved from their places where they are fixed: but they should not be fastened, as we said.
And the doctor shall place his hands on the knee with strength, until it returns: or he shall place a plank over it. Then he ties his feet to the plank until it returns.
If it does not return by this treatment, then take a plank whose length is about three arms. And hollow out a cavity in the wall which I mentioned next to the sick man, into which the end of the plank shall enter. Then place the middle of the plank on the knee. And the doctor places his feet on the other end. And bind it tightly, until the knee is straightened and returns to its place.
Then, if you wish, work with a lathe that turns with your hand: and it is so that you mark two sticks on the ground at the head of the sick man at the end of the other foot, whose length is an arm: and at his head at the end of the other foot two sticks.
The length that is between every stick [is] one palm: and now a hole has been made in every place where the tourniquet runs: and securely fasten all the sticks to the ground very much, so it does not move at all.
Insert a round stick: and there is a tourniquet in which the ligament is turned in two holes of two sticks.
At the end of it is a hole, in which the stick is fastened, whose length is one palm and a bit more [than that]: and similarly on two other sticks there are [holes] too.
Then bind the ligaments that you bound on the chest of the sick man with the tourniquet that is at his head: and bind the ligaments that you bound on his limbs in the tourniquet that is at his feet.
Then stand at every tourniquet the servant who turns his hand with a cloth, by which the tourniquet is turned: and the doctor ties the Spondilium, as we said before.
Here is the form of the tourniquet [that follows].
Then after the Spondilium returns and the place is tied, then it is necessary that you put on an ointment of dried figs with egg white. Then [apply] wool. Then place on top of the ointment a star-shaped figure from a board, whose width is three fingers or about that. And its length should be such as to cover the area of the swelling and the healthy part of the Spondilium.
Then it is tied with the ligament that I mentioned. The sick man should use a subtle food until he gets well.
If something remains protruding in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary that [they administer] medicines that soften and soothe, with the use of the board that I mentioned for a long time. And now, the leaf (or plate) of lead is administered to it.
And at times, it happens that a prominence occurs on the last vertebra of the back; and it is thought to be a dislocation, and it is the bone that protrudes. Therefore, it is not appropriate to make an assumption with this medicine; for perhaps it will cause death.
Chapter on the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
Know that the joints of the ankle and the joints of the shoulder do not happen unless there is a dislocation; nor does it happen to them what happens to the other joints from a slight removal and concavity.
Indeed, the ankle joint is dislocated in four parts. That is, because it is dislocated to the front and to the back.
Indeed, it is mostly dislocated inwardly; and it is less often dislocated to the front and to the back.
And the treatment for dislocation to the interior is: because when you connect the weak foot with a healthy one, it is longer; and the knee of this [dislocated] one protrudes more than that of the healthy one, and the weak one cannot double its foot at the groin; and there is a place following the groin which has been pressed by a hot press, because the head of the hip has already reached there.
And the sign for a dislocation to the outside is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms [described previously].
And the sign for a dislocation to the front is that it expands the healthy foot by its entire length; however, it does not double [the step] without pain in the knee; and if it tries to walk, it cannot do so forward; and urine retention occurs, and the groin area is pressed, and there is striking on the heel during walking.
And the sign for a dislocation to the back is that it does not expand the knee, nor can it double [the step] before doubling the groin; and the leg is even shorter than the other; and the groin area is softened, and the head of the hip is in its place.
The reduction [of this dislocation] is as follows: consider then if the dislocation is old, which has already healed with it. Then if you administer a reduction and it remains according to its disposition: then there is no cure at all in it. Therefore, one should not make presumption of it too deeply.
In the case where the dislocation is recent, and it belongs to one of the four types of dislocations: then proceed to this, that you wish to reduce it, and stretch it towards its interior and exterior parts, and move it right and left: perhaps it will return, and it will not need any other care.
But if it does not return in this manner, then it is necessary to prepare a strong assistant and he should extend the leg from its lower part: either with his hands or with a rope tied above the knee; and another assistant should extend his hand from above so that he introduces his hands under its joints. Then bind it with a soft rope around the root of the hip, and hold the end of another ligament.
And let the extension be from its anterior part of the groin to the breast part, or from its posterior part to the back part.
Let the extension of all [bodies] be one until the weak [body] is raised from the ground and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is a common type in the four types.
If the dislocation returns as we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for all that I will say about its cure.
In the reduction of it itself when the dislocation is towards the interior, it is necessary for the weak to lie on its healthy side. Then place a rope around the root of the hip, where it is between the head of the hip and the place that is under the groin. Then extend the rope upward from the groin to a higher part of the body, to the breast part.
Then let another strong man take hold, and enlarge the large place of the weak hip, and extend it to the outward extension with great force. Now it has returned to its own place.
And this form is easier than the other forms of cure, by which this member is restored.
If it is excised over you and does not obey you to the entrance with this form of cure at all: then it is necessary, that two weak feet are tied together with a strong ligament, loose, on both heels, and on both knees; and let the length of each be four fingers' width from its root; and let the injured leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then suspend the weak one over the head of the beam that is in the house; and let its length from the ground be two forearms' width.
Then command a strong youth to brace the head of the hip; and suspend it with another one; and let another youth push, bracing the hip with strength, for the joining returns to its own place quickly.
In its own return, when there is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the weak one lies on the platform, according to what we said in the one with humpback. And let a ligament be bound over his leg, the weak one itself, and over his chest.
Then two beams are placed, one at his feet, and another at his head. Then a beam is placed in the middle of the platform, fixed well; over which are rolled wet cloths so as not to hurt the weak one; and let the beam be between his hips, so that it is not drawn downwards at the extension.
Then let everyone go to their place, and the doctor equalizes the reduction with his hand.
If it obeys for returning, it will be good.
And if not, place it on the table and press it, as we said in gibbosity, equally. Moreover, the weak one should lie on its healthy side.
And when dislocation is towards the front, then it is necessary that the weak leg be extended quickly; and this is according to the same narrative above on a stool; and let the physician place the palm of his right hand on the thigh of the weak one. Then bend it with the other hand, and with that bending place it extended towards the lower parts at the knee.
And if dislocation is towards the back: then it is not necessary that the weak one be extended towards the lower parts and raised on the ground; instead, it should be placed on a hard surface, as it should also be when dislocation occurs to its outward side, according to what we said about lying down on two surfaces; and it should be in front of its face and the ligaments stretched; according to what we said recently.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a table also on the place from which the dislocation departs, when reduction is completed according to what is necessary.
And the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you: and it is to extend two legs of the weak one; and when you see that they are equal, and the weak one tightens his leg and stretches it without any hesitation; then you will know that the limb has returned, according to what is necessary: then join the hips, place a plaster, and tie with a bandage, which means a wheel; so that the dislocation does not move towards some part; and let the weak one remain quiet for three or four days.
Then unloose the ligament and plaster, and connect the leg with the other; then if you see that they are equal in position, then you will know that the dislocation is already fixed; therefore release the weak one for walking.
If you see something softening (rotting): then return and pack it, and bind it according to your first binding method; and leave it even for three days.
Then dissolve that (the ointment) and make it delay in walking on both sides for some days, until it recovers well.
Part of a Treatise on Dislocations Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwardly, inwardly, downwards (posteriorly), that is, backwards.
And it is not dislocated forwardly.
The sign of its dislocation is that you command the weak person to apply his leg to his hip. But if it does not adhere with the thigh: then know that the knee is dislocated.
The restoration of all the ways of dislocation is that you make the weak person sit upright, so that he now extends his leg if possible over it, and lets his back rest on your face; and place his foot between your feet.
Then make the knee adhere with your palms, and apply them straight across the knee in between the fingers. Then apply with your palm two sides of the knee with force. Let another servant extend his foot, until the knee returns to its place.
The sign of restoration is that the leg adheres with the thigh gently, not forcefully.
Then pack it (the knee), and make the leg adhere with the thigh. Then bind them together with lint for three or four days. Then dissolve it (the binding).
Let the weak person not walk for some days until he is healed.
If you are refused reduction (manual repositioning), then administer strong extension with bandages, whose method was previously mentioned in the treatment of ankles, until it returns.
Treatment for Dislocation of Heel Bone.
When the heel bone is sometimes moved slightly and other times it is dislocated completely. And its dislocation occurs either inward or outward.
The sign of its dislocation is that you see the calcaneus swollen, advancing towards the part to which it is being dislocated.
The cure for its removal (dislocation) is simple repositioning: and it is that it should be stretched easily with the hands, and made equal until it returns.
In the cure of it (dislocation) when it dislocates according to its complement, it is necessary that you make the patient sit upright: and let him be held by a strong attendant from behind the middle of his back.
Then hold with your right hand the foot from its upper part and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot at the place of the calcaneus.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with your left hand on each side (of the foot).
Then extend with your left hand and push the front of the foot with your right hand towards the calf without effort.
Do that twice as we have said.
Then push the front of the foot towards the calf on the third time and you pull the calcaneus.
If therefore it returns to its place once or twice in this manner and you see the foot made equal, that is good: but if not, then repeat the operation on it; because it will return.
If its repositioning is prevented by it as we have said, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and fix a pillow strongly in the ground so that it falls between his hips: and now roll over him a cloth to prevent the sick person from being hurt.
Then take hold of his hip by an attendant: afterwards the other attendant should stretch the leg either with hands or with a strap tied around the neck of the foot. Then all attendants should stretch against the counter stretch (of the dislocation): and let a pillow stand between the hips of the sick person holding him so that his body is not drawn towards the lower part during the stretching.
Then, the physician reduces the dislocation with his hands, and the other servant holds the sound leg below. The dislocation returns quickly.
When the dislocation returns and it is shown to you that the healing of it has taken place: then put plaster and wool, and tie it firmly with bandages, and bind the foot with a bond to the leg.
And it is necessary that you be careful or fear of the nerves which are above the heel behind, so that the bandage is not too tight and injures them.
Then release him for two or three days. If the bandage softens, tie it again: then loosen him on the third or fourth day: and prevent the weak from walking for forty days. But if he tries to walk before that time, there is no security, unless the dislocation is loosened again, and it breaks, and will not accept healing afterwards.
If a hot abscess occurs to him: then it is necessary that you administer for its soothing that which was previously told in another place about the cure, and the embrocation, until it is removed.
Chapter on Dislocation of the Toes of the Foot, and Cure.
It is necessary that what is dislocated from them be reduced with little extension without effort: and this indeed is not difficult, but rather easy.
If the dislocation is in some knots of the back of the foot: then it is necessary that the weak sits his foot on a level place of the ground, or on a table: and he stands as if walking.
Then you stand, and put your foot on that which projects from those joints. Then press it with your foot with force until it returns, and you see that it is reduced, and the projection no longer appears in its place.
Then place under his foot a board that can hold the whole foot, which has two holes. Then tighten it with a bandage for three days. Then loosen it, and let him rest from walking for many days until it is tightened and secured against displacement.
On the types of dislocations with wound or fracture.
When something like this occurs, and you attempt its treatment and restoration, death often happens many times over; and therefore, one should not presume to treat it unless he is wise in the art of long practice, merciful, pious, slow, not daring, nor presumptuous; and that he should use only medicines which assuage hot inflammations in the beginning; and let him leave the sick to divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for salvation from destruction with mildness of illness, and where there is something that appears promising. Therefore, try the reduction of it immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen that assuages the abscess; and care for the wound with it as suits it from dry ointments.
If the dislocation is with fracture and splinters separate from the bone occur, then try their removal, and do what was said before in simple diseases in those places.
And beware according to your own strength, and guard yourself from entering into the path of fear; as was said before by my preaching to you.
For it is better for you before your Creator, and more enduring to your glory, and more beneficial for your body in your time among them.
